PITTSBURGH SYMPHONY
Music for Everyone - Music for your Life
Mellon Volum e9l88,I8
Friday, February, 27, 2004 8:00 pm 
Saturday, February 28, 2004 8:00 pm 
Sunday, February, 29, 2004 2:30 pm
Pittsburgh Symphony Heinz Hall, in the heart of the Cultural District
The Music of France
Yan Pascal Tortelier, conductor 
FRANCK: Symphony in D minor 
DUTILLEUX: The Shadows of Time 
RAVEL: Bolero
Ravels Bolero- an explosion of color, sound and rhythm - shines 
at these concerts that feature the music of French composers.
Youll also discover Dutilieuxs The Shadows of Time, which was 
inspired by Paris liberation in World War II. (PS0 Spokesman Ken 
Meltzer will preview this piece before you hear it!)
Insights - Ken Meltzer previews Dutiileuxs The Shadows of Time, 
inspired by Paris liberation in World War II.
Behind The Notes Concert Preview (one hour before the concert): 
Viva la France! - The great tradition of French concert music.
STUDENT RUSH TICKETS on sale at the Pittsburgh Symphony Box Office two hours before the concert with 
a valid student ID, or buy discounted tickets in advance at the University Center information desk.

The Tartan is the student newspaper of 
Carnegie Mellon University. The first issue is 
free. Additional copies can be purchased for 
$0.25 each. It is a weekly newspaper published 
by students during the fall and spring semesters. 
It is printed by Western Pennsylvania Printing, 
Inc. Subscriptions are available for the semester 
or academic year. The editorials appearing at the 
beginning of the opinion section are the official 
opinion of The Tartan editorial board. Columns 
and political cartoons are the opinions of indi
vidual creators. The Tartan reserves the right to 
reject or condense any column or political car
toon. Letters to the Editor are opinions of 
authors who are usually, but not necessarily, 
members of the University community. A letter 
intended for publication must be signed and 
include the writers address and phone number 
for verification. Names may be withheld upon 
request. The Tartan requests that letters be no 
longer than 350 words. The Tartan reserves the 
right to condense or reject any letter. Letters to 
the Editor must be submitted by 5 pm the 
Thursday before publication. Copyright  2004 
by The Tartan. The Tartan editorial board 
reserves the right to withhold from publication 
any copy it deems unfit for publication. Office: 
University Center Room 314. Phone: (412) 268- 
2111. Mailing address: The Tartan/Box 1017/ 
Carnegie Mellon University/
Pittsburgh, PA 15289-1017.
E-mail:
tartan@andrew. emu. edu 
All rights reserved.

ISSN#:0890-3107.

Collegiate Press.______________________

APA Pageant
Asian groups sponsor students with talent and beauty
Wednesday night, Asian Heritage Week was in 
full swing with the Asian Pacific American (APA) 
pageant. Kicked off by the annual Lunar Gala 
fashion show on Valentines Day, Asian Heritage 
Week was packed full of activities to promote 
Asian awareness on campus. The purpose of the 
APA pageant, according to the Lambda Phi 
Epsilon brothers, was to showcase the talent and 
abilities available to the Asian-American college 
community through the contestants.
Still full from tofu tiramisu from the previous 
nights Iron Chef cook-off competition, the audi
ence shuffled in, greeted by Lambda boys crisply 
dressed in dark suits. The audience filled under 
a quarter of Rangos auditorium, and mostly con
sisted of members of the four Asian-interest 
Greek organizations: Lambda Phi Epsilon, Pi 
Delta Psi, Kappa Phi Lambda, and Alpha Kappa 
Delta Phi. Others audience members included 
members and friends of Asian Student 
Association (ASA), Taiwanese Student 
Association (TSA), and Hong Kong Student 
Association (HKSA).
The second annual APA pageant included 
males as well as females to compete for Mr. and 
Miss APA Pageant. There were four women con
testants  Dynne Sung, a first-year H&SS stu
dent representing Kappa Phi Lambda, Kristen 
Hwang, a first-year in music representing ASA, 
Grace Lam, a sophomore biology major repre
senting Alpha Kappa Delta Phi, and Winnifred 
Tse, a first-year design major, representing TSA. 
The male contestants were Hengyi Guo, a firstyear interdisciplinary H&SS student represent
ing Pi Delta Psi, Ricky Lam (first-year MCS stu
dent) representing HKSA, and sophomore social 
& decision sciences major Michael Ma represent
ing TSA.
The evening started late due to technical diffi
culties, and opened up with banter from the 
emcees. The schedule began with brief introduc
tions. Next was the cultural wear section, which 
showcased two of the girls with traditional 
Korean han-bok dresses and the other two in 
Chinese-style high-cut dresses. The males strut
ted out in brightly colored Chinese shirts and Ma 
received the audiences praise by sporting a 
grass skirt and a fake coconut bra.
The real meat of the show was the talent 
showcase section where each contestant pre
sented a skill that would highlight their person
alities. Lam was first with a dance number incor
porating a costume with long sleeve-like wings. 
Guo was next with a dance number as well, but
less traditional and with a bit more skin and 
booty-shaking. Hwang presented a vocal num
ber, a Korean pop song, only slightly hindered by 
technical acoustic imbalance. Lam performed a 
classical three-movement piano piece on an 
electric keyboard, Sung came out in traditional 
Korean dress and performed a piece on the 
gayageum, a traditional Korean stringed instru
ment. Ma was next to showcase his martial arts 
ability to break a solid concrete block with his 
bare hands. He succeeded to the roar of the 
crowd after his second try. Tse was the final per
former, where she skillfully applied makeup on 
one of her friends to showcase her makeover 
ability.
There was a question and answer period dur
ing which the contestants donned their formal 
wear, and fortune cookies with questions inside 
them were handed out to the contestants. 
Contained within was a single difficult question 
pertaining to diversity, culture, and campus 
awareness to which contestants had to respond.
Before the intermission, the emcees acknowl
edged their sponsors, which included Kaplan 
and STA Travel, by showing a video commercial 
created by the Lambda brothers. During the 
intermission, the audience was encouraged to 
cast their vote for Mr. and Miss APA by handing 
slips of paper to ushers with their choice. They 
were informed that 40 percent of the decision 
was determined by the audiences votes and 
sixty percent was dependent of the judges deci
sions. The panel of judges consisted of Danielle 
Gorman, from Kaplan; Emily Half, of Student 
Affairs; Yueming Yu, a Chinese language profes
sor; and Alex Duan, a senior business adminis
tration major and the current president of 
Lambda Phi Epsilon.
Kristen Hwang and Michael Ma won the con
test, coming out with the most votes and points. 
The runners-up were Dynne Sung and Ricky 
Lam. The title winners received a scholarship to 
any of their programs; the overall winner won a 
travel voucher allowing him to fly to anywhere 
in the U.S. The runners-up each received a din
ner for two at any Thai Place Restaurant in the 
Pittsburgh area.

Kaleidoscope at the Frame
A colorful display by students of the BHA and BSA schools
One of the most interesting pieces on exhibit was a 
wall filled with envelopes containing fictional invita
tions to different events and RSVP cards for people to 
fill out. This was created by senior BHAs Astrid Chow 
and Laura Conrad with the intention of being 
extremely interactive. Many people thoroughly 
enjoyed taking the time to read each invitation.
Sculptures by first-year BHA Juliet Pusateri and sen
ior BHA Zach Svigals comfortably filled the space in 
the main room of The Frame. Specifically, a sculpture 
created out of metal piping by the latter, Svigals, 
embraces a grace of industrial design.
This week at The Frame, BHA and BSA students 
have accomplished something quite significant with 
Kaleidoscope, an annual exhibition of their artwork 
and performances. Somewhere between a series of 
black and white photos by Julia Hustwit, a senior in 
BHA, which capture the simplicity of the cityscape, 
and the stuffed animal creations of sophomore BHA 
Marina Kliger, it becomes clear that this is not art cre
ated in a vacuum. There is nothing stuffy, overly intel
lectual, or artsy-fartsy about anything being shown. 
Its not pedestrian either. Maybe it is because of what 
the BHA and BSA programs allow: art within a con
text. The opportunity to work within the spheres of 
design, architecture, music, drama, science, and the 
humanities allows for authenticity.
At the opening reception on Friday, live music was 
provided by Kathleen Agres, a junior BSA who played 
the cello as students whose pieces were on exhibition 
milled about, enjoying an assortment of refreshments 
and explaining their artwork to interested peers. 
Because of the nature of Kaleidoscope, there was no 
specific theme to the show as a whole. Instead, many 
artists chose to establish a theme within a series of 
their work. Fourteen paintings by sophomore BHA 
Jennifer Keinard of cows with words on each to create 
the sentence Fourteen cows standing in a row ren
dered in the most exquisite detail serves as a perfect 
example. Also, a three-part series of oil on canvas 
paintings by Joana Ricou, senior BSA, explored the 
use of deep reds, oranges, and yellows.
viewer through its beautiful shots of cafs, a perfectly 
French town square, and tiny stone streets.
Brian Mathias, a first-year BHA, has one of his musi
cal compositions available on a walkman for listening. 
I think Kaleidoscope is a great opportunity for us to 
display work that combines what we are studying, 
said Mathias.
The Frame is open 12-5 pm, Friday through 
Tuesday. Frame opening receptions are held on 
Friday, 7-9 pm. Call 412.268.2081 for more informa
tion.

AHu,so bvliiyo,vwo
Editors Note: Marina Kliger is a former Assistant Art 
Editor for The Tartan.
Patrick Donnelly, a junior in BSA, experimented 
with light as seen through the medium of glass in a 
collection of black and white photographs and senior 
BHA Stephanie Natales color photographs of beauti
ful, old stone buildings and archways explored ele
ments of space. There were also other photo exhibits 
at the Frame on Friday.
A number of movies, created individually by sophmore Tali Blankfeld and senior Yann Seznec, both 
BHAs, were also an integral part of the exhibition. 
They vary greatly in genre and composition. Seznecs 
movie, which was filmed in Avignon, France captures 
a feel for the city and demands the attention of the
Veronique Lee/Photo Staff

Activist film festival
Opinions declared with dedication to getting the message out
The Indymedia film festival came to Porter Hall to 
inform the city of Pittsburgh of activism through 
video documentation. The Pittsburgh Independent 
Media Center, a hub of local activism which advocates 
anything protest-worthy  from unfair janitor treat
ment to President Bush  came to campus to inform, 
documentary-style, about the past activist movements 
that worked and changed U.S. policies.
On Thursday, the film festival showcased historical 
perspectives of activism with documentary works by 
Paper Tiger TV, Not Channel Zero, Testing the Limits, 
and Access Denied.
The first documentary, World? What World? Keeping 
America Disinformed, the World According to U.S. 
Media, was done by Paper Tiger TV, a non-profit, vol
unteer video collective located in New York City. It 
was a video piecing together several clips of different 
news broadcasts on TV as well as newspaper clip
pings. It had a very homemade feel with hand-paint
ed backgrounds, hand-written scribbles, and lowquality audio and film.
The message was strong: that all media is pretty 
much run by the American government. They recited 
statistics about how 70 percent of the world media is 
owned by the U.S., and how all the U.S. media is 
owned by General Electric, who they say takes pay
offs from the U.S. government.
The second documentary was an episode of Media 
Chef, produced by Not Channel Zero. Media Chef is an 
African-American man with a chef hat and apron and 
a large video camera, reporting to the audience about 
things in the media that are questionable. This 
episode was about the TV show Cops. He rode in a real 
police car and taped his own Cops show and discov
ered the recipe for a successful real-life drama show: 
a little bit of shaking the camera, a pinch of violence, 
and voil! A television hit. The documentary was a 
short Media Chef episode, about 10 minutes running 
time. Not Channel Zero is an African-American and 
Latino collective based in New York City.
The third documentary was Voices from the Front, 
an award-winning documentary about AIDS activism 
produced by the Testing the Limits Collective. This 
was the longest documentary. It ran about two hours, 
but it was professionally produced and very com
pelling. The basic focus of this documentary is the 
ACT UP activism group, and how this entity shouted 
and actually changed the government policies on 
AIDS. The acronym is for AIDS Coalition to Unleash 
Power; ACT UP is a diverse, non-partisan group of 
individuals united in anger and committed to direct 
action to end the AIDS crisis.
I
The mission statement says it all when it comes to
this documentary: ACT UP, a collective body founded 
in 1987 of angry, adamant people with AIDS, rose to 
fight the roadblocks to AIDS treatment, medication, 
research, and general care for all people, including 
fighting the exclusion of women and people of color. 
While it started in New York City, ACT UP has spread 
out all over the U.S. and the world, including 
Pittsburgh and Paris. ACT UP was listed as one of the 
top ten most influential social justice organizations in 
the United States during the past 30 years, according 
to inthefray.com, an online magazine, in 2003,
The last documentary show was a video produced 
by Access Denied, a 26-minute video simply tided 
Abortion. It told the story of radical Christian funda
mentalists who physically discouraged women from 
entering abortion clinics, and the reactions of the clin
ic workers. This documentary was to inform the audi
ence about the dangers of illegal abortion, and to 
show how the anti-abortionists, such as Operation 
Rescue, are dealing with this issue. There is a very 
one-sided spin to this video editorial; however it does 
present the audience with at least one perspective of 
the issue.
Indymedia is a collective of independent media 
organizations and hundreds of journalists offering 
grassroots, non-corporate coverage. Their website 
describes the organization as a democratic media 
outlet for the creation of radical, accurate, and pas
sionate tellings of truth.
Visit videofest.indypgh.org for more information and 
the schedule.

A perfect crescendo
Soloist graces the Philharmonic
It was supposed to be an evening promising nothing 
but grandeur and impressive performance. A worldclass soloist, the Universitys top ensemble, and some 
of the greatest orchestral repertoire of the 20th centu
ry came together last Thursday. Much to the satisfac
tion of all in the audience, the ensemble managed to 
deliver a performance that was nothing short of bril
liant.
Under the direction of conductor Juan Pablo 
Izquierdo, the Carnegie Mellon Philharmonic present
ed a concert of 20th-century music at Carnegie Music 
Hall. On the program were Maurice Ravels Rapsodie 
Espagnole, Ottorino Respighis Pini di Roma, and 
Romance for Cello and Orchestra by College of Fine 
Arts head Alan Fletcher. The guest soloist was Andrs 
Daz, Artist in Residence at the Brevard Music Center 
in North Carolina.
A very complex piece, Romance provided a demon
stration of the orchestras skill and agility. In addition 
to Diazs brilliant solo work, the ensemble backed him 
flawlessly. Intermediate solos for the ensembles prin
cipal string players mirrored the emotion in the cello. 
Such collaboration managed to highlight the intrica
cies of the piece: the composer and conductor seemed 
to strike a perfect balance between featuring the 
soloist and featuring the orchestra, with each playing 
off the other in perfect harmony.
After the intermission, the program finished with a 
rendition of Respighis powerful Pines of Rome. 
Written in 1924, the tone poem contains vivid musical 
imagery of the Roman countryside ranging from a 
busy village to a dark catacomb. An offstage solo and 
a surprise entrance from a brass sextet in the upper 
balcony combined to add even more variation.
be for the third movement of Pines: a recorded 
nightingale, something called for in the score, seemed 
to stick out too much and was a bit obvious for the 
performance hall. Additionally, many parts of the 
suite were a bit underblown: while the raucous parts 
of the last movement were powerful enough to rattle 
pocket objects, some of Respighis most powerful 
musical strokes were lost.
By the end of the night, the audience, consisting 
mostly of CMU students, was suitably impressed. One 
question remained on many minds, however: with 
music this enjoyable, why arent more people jam
ming the concert hall at every opportunity?
A,2 MgH98N 
rlul-,u- Tm,vso
The Ravel piece was performed masterfully, from its If any criticism can come of the performance, it 
small, quiet beginning to its triumphal climax and 
back down to an ending just as unassuming as the 
opening. The orchestra nailed every nuance, change, 
and complexity that the piece had to offer. It was a 
great way to open the concert, but the star of the show 
was yet to come.
Perhaps the reason for everyones attendance was 
for the second item on the program, Alan Fletchers 
Romance with Diaz playing solo cello. It was the 
Pittsburgh premiere of the piece; in fact, there had 
only been one other public performance ever. More 
than a mere matching of player and composition, the 
piece has additional history to make it ever more 
important, according to Fletcher.
The cell was composed for Andrs, wrote Fletcher 
in an e-mail. It exists also in a version for cello and 
piano and that has been performed quite a bit, but the 
orchestra version has only been given twice  in 
Boston and here, both times played by Andrs Daz.
Furthermore, theres an astounding amount of 
shared history between composer, performer, and 
even the conductor. According to Fletcher, Izquierdo 
was conducting the Santiago (Chile) Philharmonic in 
the 1980s when Diazs father was that orchestras 
principal viola player. Diaz and Fletcher then met 
when Diaz came to the New England Conservatory, 
where Fletcher was dean.
That acquaintance became the genesis for the piece; 
one summer, Fletcher began to write a cello piece on 
Diazs suggestion while visiting a villa in Tuscany. 
Unfortunately, the piece ended up taking nearly a year 
to complete due to the failing health of the friend with 
whom Fletcher spent the summer.
Jim Puls/Managing Editor

Fuzzy math
Serious Money satirizes the greed of the 80s
The Carnegie Mellon University School of Drama 
opened the play Serious Money last Thursday at the 
Philip Chosky Theater. The play, written entirely in 
verse by Caryl Churchill, is a satire about high finance 
in 1980s London after the biggest deregulation of the 
London stock market in modern times, also known as 
the Big Bang.
Jake Todd (played by Mark Elliott Silverberg) and 
his sister Scilla (Erin McLemore) both work at the 
London International Financial Futures and Options 
Exchange. One day, Jake is found shot to death, and 
his death is ruled a suicide. Scilla believes otherwise 
and takes it upon herself to solve his murder. Her 
quest for vengeance, however, becomes a greedy 
treasure hunt when she discovers he has made a mil
lion pounds through insider trading.
At the same time, corporate raider Billy Corman 
(Seth Fisher) is trying to take over a small, failing 
business run by Duckett (Ian Christiansen). Billy was 
involved in Jakes dirty deal and wants the whole 
thing kept quiet until he closes his own deal. He is 
supported in his efforts by Jacinta Condor, a beauti
ful Peruvian businesswoman who makes her money 
off copper and puts it all in cocaine plantations and 
Eurobonds. Zac Zackerman, an American stockbro
ker who seems to know everyone and everything that 
is going on, provides all of the narrative and exposi
tion. Thats more than one can say for the audience.
Scenes change quickly; the chronology is a trial to 
follow and the machine-gun dialogue is riddled with 
British slang and financial terms that are sometimes 
hard to understand. The plot, however, is more ele
mentary. Serious Money is about avarice and the pain 
it causes. The characters all just want to make money 
for the sake of making even more money. As Jacinta 
says with tacit simplicity, I do not want to help. I 
want to be rich.
Serious Money features several standout perform
ances. Seth Fisher provides Billy Corman  a charac
ter who could just as well be played with complete 
one-dimensionality  with unexpected depth and 
authenticity. Polly Harrison is memorable as Marylou 
Baines, an American arbitrageur with ice water run
ning through her veins. The play makes use of double 
casting, with some of the actors playing top financial 
bigwigs in some scenes and floor-bound underlings 
in others. In a play with rapid dialogue and a nonlin
ear plot, this could be bewildering.
The quality of the acting, however, prevents any 
confusion. J.D. Goldblatt is especially good. His two 
characters, old tightwad businessman Jack Merrison 
and mysterious con artist Nigel Ajibala, are so distinct 
and well-played that they may as well have been two
different people.
The set design is simple and elegant, with a single 
spiral staircase and balcony acting as the central 
piece. The costumes are reminiscent of the 1980s and 
hair and makeup are absolutely fantastic. Serious 
Money contains two unexpected but not out-of-place 
musical numbers. The first, which takes place on the 
floor and involves a bunch of male and female 
traders, is both startling and grotesque. It is not play
ful or cute; it is raw and frantic and ugly. The scene 
maintains such intensity that the overall effect is 
comical. These people take themselves so seriously, 
but how can we help but laugh at a chorus line of 
people with bad 80s hair and power suits chanting, I 
fucked it with my backers! This scene elicited a neg
ative response in an entire row of elderly patrons, 
who got up and left during the intermission.
Although Serious Money spends most of its energy 
satirizing British high finance in particular, it easily 
skewers America as well. Indeed, Caryl Churchills
message will ring true for most any capitalist country 
eager to jump on the global economy bandwagon. 
One can enjoy the hyperbole of Serious Money with
out knowing much about the stock market. Simply 
put, it is a play about greedy rich people who are 
emotionally destitute. In general, Serious Money is an 
enjoyable play with a talented cast. Although the 
show staggers a bit towards the finish line, audiences 
are sure to enjoy its dry humor and larger-than-life 
characters.
Serious Money will be showing at the Philip Chosky 
Theater in the Purnell Center for the Arts February 
24-28. Directed by Steven Cosson. For tickets and 
information, call 412.268.2407.

Wall Scrawl
From the stalls to the halls
Forms of expression run rampant on campus, 
including everything from the constant bickering 
over political issues on misc.market to the seemingly 
ubiquitous t-shirts emblazoned with Greek letters. 
However, some of the most frequently seen state
ments occur in the form of writing found in all differ
ent areas of campus, in places where writing general
ly isnt supposed to be. Bathroom scribbling may not 
be the most desirable thing to look at, but it does get 
seen every time someone takes a trip to the can  
actually making it a surprisingly effective mode of 
communication. Some ways of going about this writ
ing are considered vandalism; though, Carnegie 
Mellon has approved other methods of getting stu
dents messages out. Along with the more mundane 
methods of spouting out notes or advertising events, 
there are examples of artistic and historic graffiti to 
be considered as well. The walls of the campus visi
ble to passers-by are relatively graffiti-free, but on a 
closer look it is easy to see that members of the cam
pus community do show interest in all sorts of artistic 
wall decorations.
Barbara Mackenzie-Wood, head of the acting/musi
cal theater program at CMU, believes that its quite 
inspiring to go and look at the walls and see the lega
cy that came before you. There are reproductions of 
seventy-year-old signatures and illustrations; seeing 
the lightheartedness and humor of ages past can have 
a profound effect on the viewer. It is also interesting to 
see the famous alumni who have signed the walls over 
the years, including Blair Underwood, Ted Danson, 
Robbie Marshall, and Holly Hunter. Holly Hunters 
chunk of wall is on display on the second floor of 
Purnell; she was one of the first to break the first-year 
curse of being cut after signing the wall early. The wall 
is unique from one end to the other. Its fun to see 
how each class has chosen to present themselves, 
said Mackenzie-Wood.
Usually, though, walls are painted with less legiti
macy. Margaret Morrison Carnegie Hall is the second 
oldest building on campus, originally built in 1906, 
and its two sub-basements are covered in all forms of 
graffiti. Real, New York-subway-style graffiti adorns 
the walls; the mix of highly stylized signatures, witty 
sayings, and initials of countless organizations gives 
the underground passages in Margaret Morrison a 
very gritty, urban feel.
Part of the sub-basement area is used for buggy 
team storage; several buggy- and carnival-centric 
organizations use the space. So, not surprisingly, a lot 
of the markings on the walls are logos and names of 
organizations. Fringe, KGB, CIA, and others all make 
appearances several times on the walls.
There are more eclectic messages scrawled on the 
walls, though  ominous notes that seem more at 
home in a suspense movie than on a college campus. 
As the hallway turns a corner, TURN BACK NOW! 
can be seen, messily drawn in thick black spray paint. 
The symbol from the Blair Witch movies is painted in 
several places, no doubt the work of a student with 
not quite enough creativity. Elsewhere, Help! is 
seemingly hastily scribbled. Other students chose to 
put their philosophical ramblings on display; one stu
dent wrote Existence is Senseless, while someone 
else decided to honor the famous philosopher 
Frederich Nietzsche by spray-painting his name on a 
wall.
Not every message on the walls of the Margaret 
Morrison sub-basements has a deep meaning, though.
Anupama Melville/Art Staff
Graffiti as an art form, even an unintentional art 
form, is quite different from what one might find 
etched into desks. For example, each year, graduat
ing members of the Carnegie Mellon school of drama 
cordon off a segment of the fire curtains backstage at 
the Chosky Theater in the Purnell Center. The stu
dents illustrate their section of the curtains by paiqting their names, quotes, and various artistic designs 
to lend personality. In fact, the tradition began 
decades before the Purnell Center was even designed. 
As early as 1920, graduates of the school of drama 
took sections of the backstage area in the Kresge 
Theater and imbued them with history. When the 
school of drama moved to the Purnell Center, each 
years section of wall was photographed and repro
duced at full size, and the pieces now hang along the 
hallway and lobby walls all over the building.
pillbox. Sc iTeh,Ahp Soort

---------------------------------------------------

Veronique Lee/Photo Staff
This cryptic message of love between a bus and an unknown was left in Weans eighth floor. Wean was one of the more defaced buildings on campus.
Lighthearted phrases such as Smoke pot! and Go 
Fringe, beat PiKA! break the generally dark tone of 
the art. All the graffiti lends atmosphere to the sub
basements  even though they are in a part of cam
pus that is not usually frequented, there is not really 
any other place to find surroundings like that. The 
area also speaks of the wide range of possibilities for 
what students chose to immortalize.
In a more public venue, the Fence is continually cov
ered in some form of artistic expression. The Fence on 
the Cut is one of the longest-standing traditions in 
CMU graffiti history. Erected by the class of 1923 as a 
point of congregation, the Fence became a place for 
students to write messages to others through the nowfamiliar custom of painting the fence. Over the years, 
a system of rules and protocols were established to 
make sure the tradition would be able to last. The 
Fence became the most-painted object in the world 
with more than 15,000 coats of paint before it col
lapsed under its own weight in the 1980s. A new con
crete and steel fence was built soon after the collapse 
and it still stands today, still embodying very public 
expressions on campus.
Date, the Fence was hijacked by a group of stu
dents calling themselves the Jakaz. They used spray 
paint directly over the coat that was already there, 
writing over Valentines Day messages. The students, 
who wish to remain anonymous, justified their actions 
via e-mail by saying We did everyone a favor. 
Valentines Day was over and noone [sic] wants to see 
Valentines Day ads after the fact. This was a case of 
pure-and-simple graffiti, with anarchy symbols, selec
tions from Dr. Seuss The Lorax, and a riddle scribbled 
in black and red spray paint. It is against fence regula
tions to use spray paint to paint the fence; the perpe
trators acknowledged this by saying, Paint is paint 
people, get over it. If you dont like it, feel free to 
(spray) paint over it. The vandals also used the 
opportunity to advertise what they felt to be the last
good concert ever to be shown at Laga, Poison the 
Well.
If you do not attend this concert, you do not know 
what youre doing with your life, said one of the 
Jakaz.
In the lavatories around campus, students try their 
hand at very different types of writing on the walls. 
Students might enjoy an occasional bathroom limer
ick or they may find them to be an eyesore, but the 
doodles wont be going away anytime soon. On CMUs 
campus there are far more things written in mens 
rooms, which is most likely the trend everywhere. 
Some of the best written stall-scrawl can be found in 
CFAs fifth floor mens room and in Weans eighth
floor restroom. Memorable lines from these locales 
include, Why are the Steelers like a tampon? B/C 
they are only good for one period and dont have a sec
ond string, as well as diatribes on the difficulties of 
getting things out. Womens rooms have consider
ably fewer markings; investigations of womens rest
rooms in Porter, Doherty, Wean, CFA and others yield
ed next to nothing.
As it turns out, though, the word graffiti may be a 
bit of a misnomer. According to junior math major 
Jeffrey Guarente, If you want to see your average 
bathroom art, go to CMU, but calling it graffiti, ... is 
insulting to the graf community.
Carnegie Mellon has a set of rules and regulations to 
differentiate between semi-permanent markings that 
are tolerated in certain areas, and things that are con
sidered vandalism. Chalking, which is very common 
during the warmer months of the year, is allowed as 
long as the markings are made with nonpermanent 
substances on horizontal outdoor surfaces that are 
exposed to the elements. This allows students a great 
deal of liberty regarding what they can do to get their 
messages across, but still maintains order and cleanli
ness on campus.
The University does a good job of making sure that 
most illegitimate graffiti gets cleaned up fast, but no 
one can cover everything. Taking an afternoon off to 
explore campus and see some of the interesting writ
ing and art is a great time investment, and it offers a 
glimpse of Carnegie Mellons personality with a very 
different spin.

Game Boys just want to have fun
Handface rocks out in the joy of the moment
This must be how people felt the first time they 
heard Jimi Hendrix play.
A year and a half ago, eight-bit geek rock band 
Handface formed when senior music and anthropolo
gy BHA student Seznec united with junior design 
majors Jon Brodsky and Alberto Almarza. Their lead 
instrument is an atomic purple Game Boy Color.
I play Game Boy on all songs, but for some of the 
songs I have a keyboard controller, said Brodsky. He 
means a computer keyboard, not a piano-like key
board.
The Game Boy musical software was written by 
Role Model and is also used by a New York artist 
named NullSleep. Pittsburghs 8 Cylinder plays sever
al Game Boys, but with different software.
Every Handface instrument holds a lesson in uncon
ventionality. Handface drags a small army of instru
ments, amps, and a bucket of miscellaneous acces
sories to their live shows.
Seznec started off using a bass guitar but broke it 
after he beat it with drumsticks. He replaced it with a 
guitar that he bought for $15 at a yard sale.
Seznec supplements the guitar sound by plugging it 
into a multi-effects box. Some output from the box 
goes to special effects guitar pedals. The rest goes 
through a digital 8-track that pitch-shifts the guitarplaying to sound one octave lower. It gets funneled 
through a bass amp to give the impression of what 
Seznec called a ghost bassist.
Almarzas percussion includes everything from an 
old drum kit to two Sonic Smash pedals, a tire rim, a 
lost student art project, a tambourine, and what his 
bandmates said resembles a stove burner.
Alberto loves finding random shit to hit, said 
Seznec.
Handfaces music lacks vocals or anything that 
would firmly identify them with specific bands or 
genres. They are rooted in what Brodsky described as 
structure, rhythm, and whatever.
The music takes off from Brodskys playing a pat
terned melody and beat from his Game Boy reper
toire. Seznec and Almarza improvise special effects 
guitar noises and abstract percussion. Beyond expla
nation, it somehow all works.
The one standout marker about Handface is that 
they embody music at Carnegie Mellon.
[Our art is] a reflection of the crazy times we live 
in, explained Seznec. Its perfect post-modern music
 this really crazy, digitized, lo-fi music. I think thats 
what life is about now in a lot of sectors in the world, 
at least in our tech-school mentality.
Handface might be a trio of artsy majors, but the 
band still appeals to the computer-techie nature with 
their Game Boy-dominated style. They love to yell 
best ending ever as songs conclude, which is a 
Simpsons reference. They have songs titled Smells 
Like Headphones and Copy of Smells Like 
Headphones, a joke that will only make sense to peo
ple who use Pro-Tools software.
My parents told me we should tour all the techschools, joked Seznec.
All the tech-schools with an art department! 
added Brodsky.
Seznec has a concentration in music and a minor in 
music technology, but another shout-out to CMU 
music is the analogy between Game Boy music and 
our beloved bagpipes. Bagpipes produce a coarse but 
high-pitched screeching wail. They have inspired 
Brodskys musical contribution to at least one 
Handface song. [Game Boy] can emit painful nois
es, noted Brodsky.
Perhaps Handfaces biggest geek joke is the title of 
their new album ... did you mean handface? The title 
is what pops up first when someone types Handface 
into Google.
The album is Handfaces fourth venture into the 
world of recording. It was recorded in a studio in CFA, 
as opposed to its predecessors which were recorded in 
Seznecs living room. Released earlier this month,... 
did you mean hand face? is certain to expand your 
musical horizons for the better.
Song titles and content are fairly arbitrary, and the 
band members themselves are unable to identify 
which song is which.
Whats the song called where I play that keyboard 
part thats cool? asked Brodsky when asked for his 
favorite song. After Seznec played the CD for him, he 
affirmed that it is the albums last track, Wing Flaps 
Functioning.
The standout song on the album is the epic The 
Exits are Located. It weaves together appealing riffs 
with momentous drumming. The song features a sam
ple that sounds like it says get the f-ck out.
I think its saying get down or check this out,  
said Brodsky. One of the two, Im not really sure.
We like making music that nobody has ever heard 
before, boasts the bands web site. While they pre
sume that other artists are playing similar music 
somewhere in the world, the band prides itself in its 
unusual sound.
Handface walks a fine line between innovative cre
ativity and a joke thats lasted longer than expected. 
The band members are conflicted over the quality of 
their work, and decry it from being unique to avoid 
sounding pretentious. They have had some limited 
success, winning them gigs at such off-campus venues 
as The Quiet Storm and Rex Theatre last week.
We dont take ourselves seriously at all, said 
Brodsky.
They are either living for the moment or attempting 
to rewrite music history. Either way, Handface consis
tently gets fans dancing around and rocking out, and 
then humming Game Boy melodies on the way home.
Handface will play several shows at The Quiet 
Storm next month.

bvliiyo,vwo
Courtesy of Handface

Final Fantasy Crystal Chronicles
Pocket-draining multiplayer
lYtmon
Courtesy of Square Enix Co., Ltd.
While Crystal Chronicles does have a single-play
er mode, it truly shines in multiplayer. Four play
ers can connect to the GameCube through cables 
that connect to Game Boy Advances (GBAs). 
Unlike Gauntlet characters, Crystal Chronicles 
characters do not level up; instead, they quest for 
artifacts and upgrade their items to improve 
their stats.
This makes character growth a much more fluid 
affair than killing that last monster for the everimportant experience. The gameplay also shifts 
goals away from personal achievement and inte
grates them more into the storyline, which does 
not fail to impress.
The player starts in his hometown, emerging 
into a world whose atmosphere is saturated with a 
life-draining substance known as miasma. Only 
the power of giant crystals keeps the miasma at 
bay. These crystals must be recharged with myrrh, 
a substance obtained from rare trees.
Unfortunately, myrrh also seems to attract mon
sters of the largest variety  bosses. Once youve 
defeated their minions, they alone stand between 
you and the myrrh your town eagerly awaits. This 
yearly quest organizes the game into chapters (chroni
cled in your diary), with areas regenerating myrrh 
every other year.
Combat centers around a chalice, which both holds 
the myrrh you collect and protects against miasma. 
Players start to lose health once they travel beyond its 
protective sphere, which keeps the party together. The 
chalice is also imbued with one of four elements 
(earth, fire, wind, and water), providing the party 
with increased resistance against those kinds of 
attacks. The chalices alignment also allows you to 
travel through miasma streams that crisscross the 
world, dividing it into five sections.
ent weaknesses and strengths in Square Enixs multi
player design. Yes, a Game Boy Advance is needed to 
accommodate more than one player. In fact, everyone 
playing must own one, making four-player participa
tion a costly affair for those uninitiated into the 
portable platform. Also, if you are an SP owner, make 
sure the Game Boy connection cable you buy is com
patible.
The cables designed for the first version of the 
Advance cover up the L and R buttons on top of 
the Game Boy. It is possible to use those cables by 
not inserting them all the way, but its unwieldy 
and gives the player the feeling that the cord may 
pop out at any time.
For all the tribulations of ownership and acces
sories, the multiplayer experience is a dynamic 
and rewarding one. Each player is provided a dif
ferent map on their GBA screen, giving them 
information they must share with the other party 
members to be more effective. The personal 
screen also lets players manage their own items, 
equipment, and shopping concurrently with the 
other players, making it a nearly seamless affair. 
The separate display means that screen real 
estate is not taken up by someone switching one 
spell for another in the middle of a battle.
It is too bad that this game assumes a hard
ware investment far beyond that of a typical 
GameCube owner. Though only one copy of 
Crystal Chronicles is needed for four players, this 
means that the GameCube owner will buy 
Crystal Chronicles and the link cable and try to 
find three friends with GBAs who are willing to 
buy link cables for themselves. The friends do 
not receive any further benefits from these 
cables, so a $50 game ends up running $110 
spread over four people (and even that assumes 
that they all already have the GBAs).
Although the annual quest for myrrh starts to 
become repetitive, the storyline continually drops 
hints of a larger quest, setting the stage with a rival 
(the Black Knight) and a goal  to rid the world of 
miasma.
Technically, the game does well on the GameCube. 
The graphics are exquisitely detailed and its a shame 
that the cameras wide angle bars the player from
viewing those intricately designed costumes and faces, 
except during cut-scenes. Still, the camera rarely gets 
in the way of the action like many of the Gauntlet 
clones for other systems.
It is also an experience just to cross a miasma 
stream. As the player bears the chalice through the 
stream, the miasma seems to nearly break through the 
chalices protective shield but is repulsed in an awe
some display of power. The sound also transports the 
player into the game world, as the rush of some inde
finable substance courses over your seemingly tiny 
party.
It would, however, be remiss to leave out the inher
Even so, multiplayer RPGs designed as well as 
this one only come around once every blue 
moon. Four players may be best, but even two 
players leave a great impression. Final Fantasy Crystal 
Chronicles gets eight out of 10 moogles.
James Auwaerter and Alex Meseguer spent only $65 to 
play two-player on the Cube. Swing by the Tartan office 
with your GBA and link cable for some hot multiplayer 
action.
Alex Meseguer 
Editor-in-Chief

Although Square Enix has been releasing titles fur
ther and further from their traditional formulaic suc
cesses, none have been such a complete break as Final 
Fantasy Crystal Chronicles. Square Enixs return to the 
Nintendo platform has been eagerly anticipated, but 
its unorthodox style may disappoint traditionalist fans 
of the Final Fantasy series.
Rather than following traditional role-playing game 
(RPG) conventions, Crystal Chronicles Gauntlettype gameplay de-emphasizes the battle system 
usually present in the Final Fantasy series. 
Gauntlets fast-paced action-oriented gameplay 
sucked many quarters from gamers pockets when 
it was released back in 1985. Its primary draw was 
the ability to play with up to three of your friends 
at a time - something rarely seen in RPGs then or
now.

Not Quite Kosher
Al Franken comes to Pittsburgh
On Saturday, February 21, Al Franken came to town. 
Sort of.
Students and Pittsburgh residents flocked to 
Carnegie Music Hall on Saturday night to see Franken, 
one of the original Saturday Night Live alumni as well 
as a notable political satirist, along with the Chicago 
comedy troupe The Second City. The show, titled Not 
Quite Kosher, was sponsored by the Sandford N. 
Robinson Memorial Lecture Series and produced in 
cooperation with the Hillel Jewish University Center 
and the United Jewish Federation.
As it turned out, the title of the show was apt, for 
more reasons than the producers would probably be 
happy to admit.
The show started off with an unannounced interac
tive game show entitled Is It Kosher? At the same 
time, half the audience checked their ticket stubs to see 
if they had somehow ended up at the wrong Jewish 
comedy presentation. Students from both University of 
Pittsburgh and CMU were invited to go up to the stage 
to answer trivia on Jewish celebrities and history. It 
was a nice start to the evening, designed to get the 
audience involved, but unfortunately it lasted for too 
long. By the last few rounds certain members of the 
audience were actually booing.
Thankfully, the crowds good mood recovered when 
Second City appeared next. The five-member troupe, 
which has boasted such former members as the late 
great Chris Farley and Gilda Radner, thoroughly enter
tained the audience with roughly forty-five minutes of 
improv and sketch comedy. Most of the sketches were 
directed at the Jewish members of the audience, but by
far the most popular skit was a battle for a high-school 
senior by students at both CMU and Pitt.
What was surprising about the show was that despite 
the quality of the acts, the production was startlingly 
sloppy. Sound quality and lighting were poor enough 
that if a performer moved so much as a foot away from 
center stage, they risked being in the dark and com
pletely unheard. The microphones malfunctioned at 
least once during each act, and there were awkward 
five-minute intervals between each performance.
After Second City, there was a brief presentation by 
members of Carnegie Mellons school of drama; at 
least, this is everyones guess. The presentation was 
completely unbilled, unannounced, and not explained 
afterwards, another anomaly in the evenings lineup. It 
was also two hours into the show, by which point audi
ence members were getting antsy. Some started to 
leave. The presentation focused on what appeared to 
be a Jewish day school; however, because of the lack of 
explanation and poor sound, no one was really sure 
what it was. Most of the jokes were targeted at Jewish 
stereotypes, some of which were far too narrow for the 
audience to understand. However, credit should be 
given to the students who took part  they had plenty 
of energy, and scored some big laughs.
Finally, Al Franken arrived, to everyones delight and 
relief.
In recent years Mr. Franken has developed new fame 
as a liberator of the left, and the hour or so that he 
was on the stage was a combination of stand-up come
dy and political lecture. It had absolutely nothing to do 
with being Jewish, with the exception of a brief com
mentary on Jerry Falwells declaration that the 
Antichrist is a male Jew. (I do not think anyone is in 
doubt of what Franken had to say about this.) The rest 
of his time was spent ruminating on George W. Bush, 
his recent USO tour around Iraq, his lawsuit with Fox 
News Corporation, and his recent book Lies and the 
Lying Liars That Tell Them: A Fair and Balanced Look 
at the Right. Mr. Franken certainly lives up to his title 
as liberator of the left, but he does it in a way that is 
oddly refreshing.
Yes, he does rip Republicans to shreds, and does it 
with about as much delicacy as a Chicago butcher. But 
he also does his homework, and lends credibility to 
almost all of his statements by quoting his research. In 
his plain suit and glasses, standing at a podium, he 
looked more like an English professor than a comedian, 
and his low-key demeanor only made his humor all the 
more biting. He left his audience with eerily true 
images of Saddams gold-plated front foyer and John 
Kerry dressed as a pirate, talking to his pet parrot Petey 
in a campaign ad.
All in all, it was worth the two and a half hours 
beforehand to see Mr. Franken and The Second City. 
Those who organized and promoted this event for 
months should be congratulated for their hard work. 
However, with such a long prep time, it is surprising 
that the organizers could not come up with a tighter 
production. The whole, it seemed, was not quite the 
sum of all of its parts.

It was late at night and there was a storm beginning outside. O
The wind was bending the trees to and fro and Rupert could 
hear thunder rumbling in the distance. Little did he know J
there was something stirring inside as well. There was a soft 
knock at the door. The feathers on the back of Ruperts neck stood up as if at 
attention. He didnt answer the door right away. He thought it might have just 
been the first few oversized raindrops hitting the side of the asylum. But then the 
tapping on the door was repeated. Rupert went over and opened it a crack. He
recognized the visitor as his conspiracy-spouting friend, Kent. Kent ducked into
the room and closed the door quietly behind him. If the nurses found him wan
dering the halls this late, both ducks would be in deep trouble. Rupert looked at 
his friend, scared. If they find you here.... Dude, Kent interrupted in a 
hushed but urgent voice, I couldn't sleep. There is something I have to tell you!Harold and the Other Guy a: BYa RYit

Photo courtesy of darkhorse.com
Wow, good thing she aimed high
Photo courtesy of darkhorse.com
No, its Crush #3, not The Blair Witch Project
Comickaze:
One, Two, Three
One week off. Two comic book reviews. Both 
comic books are issue #3 of their respective series. 
Now the year is 2004, which could very well cover 
the next number. However, since comics carry a 
cover date months ahead of their actual release, the 
month of April (the cover date for at least one of the 
two comics reviewed) could stand in for the num
ber four. The enjoyment factor of these comic tales 
should rate at a 5 out of 5. Not to mention the com
bined cover prices of these comic books resulting in 
a total of $5.94, which will (after tax) cost a little 
over six bucks. Okay, seriously, enough of this  on 
with the reviews:
Crush #3, Dark Horse Comics
One girl, with two different people inside of her. 
Three friends working together to solve the myste
rious transition from depressed teenager into vio
lent monster. Think Buffy meets Dr. Jekyll and Mr. 
Hyde. The star of Crush is Liz, a high schooler with 
a mild interest in magic who gets a surprise on her 
18th birthday. Imagine the shaggy-haired girl from 
The Breakfast Club discovering she was the next 
Incredible Hulk.
Liz has a crush on her best friend Jens brother 
Carl. This turns out to be convenient for everyone 
when Liz suddenly becomes a raving beast every 
time she spills a drop of blood. Kicked out of her 
house and hiding from multiple enemies, Liz, Jen 
and Carl are seeking answers behind the recent 
transformations. Enter issue #3, where Liz is obvi
ously being hunted by a powerful third party who 
knows more about the monster inside of her than 
she does. Not to mention that Liz finds out her par
ents are not her real parents. And the next day at 
school... guess who wants to make a guest appear
ance?
Each issue of Crush has been a thoroughly enjoy
able read from the front cover to the last page of 
story. The consistency of the series is unusual in 
todays climate of quick creator turnovers  a com
pelling tale with crisp art and the most striking col
ors for every issue. Crush is also distinguished by 
the fact that it is a title aimed more towards female 
readership than other action books. Most impor
tantly, the series serves what every good comic 
cooks up: a large portion of action and suspense 
with a side order of soap opera.
Plastic Man #P, DC Comics
One incredible cartoonist takes a second tier 
character and delivers three great reasons to read 
this comic: loads of laughs, lots of spandex, and 
long on great art. Its only appropriate that the 
introduction to this review should be stretched to
the limits of the 1, 2, 3 analogy. Plastic Man is the 
most elastic hero in the DC Universe.
The bendable hero is depicted monthly by Kyle 
Baker, currently one of the craziest creators in 
comics. Bakers output has reached new highs in 
the past year, delivering the socially charged Truth 
mini at Marvel and self-publishing his own collec
tions of cartoons (with more series in the works). 
Plastic Man is simply another addition to the tower
ing towers of towering comic art achievement 
which Baker achieves. Not to mention that the new 
Plastic Man series is a fantastic blend of super
heroes and slapstick animation. With the energy of 
violent cartoons such as Itchy & Scratchy, and the 
immediacy of guest stars like Superman and 
Wonder Woman, how can Plastic Man go wrong?
But thats just the problem. Issue #3 finds the 
stretchable sleuth in a tight spot, as his criminal 
past catches up to him. A beautiful FBI agent has 
come to arrest the reformed criminal once known 
as Eel OBrian. Gags galore are in store as Plastic 
Man, a.k.a. the wanted criminal once known as Eel 
OBrian, attempts to avoid the long arm of the law. 
A fantastic guest spot featuring the Justice League 
of America standing around doing absolutely zilch 
seals Plastic Man #3 as one righteous read.
Baker is a one-man band on the title, handling all 
writing and art chores. The only other credit goes 
to editor Joey Cavalieri, who probably has to 
stretch his efforts to find anything to do for this 
comic (but seriously thats an unfair assumption on 
the part of the reviewer). The only safe assumption 
here is that this review is being needlessly stretched 
out to accommodate more stretch puns and 
Plastic Man plays on words. Just go buy the comic. 
It wont a stretch a college students wallet too far.
R,Id rlo,usN 
bwu,so es2,I8 bvlii
